Book and Newspaper Introductions

The Vanguard Weekly 195

— Tang Bin

Issues 18 and 19

Besides the polemic on nationalism and poetry by Tang Bingruo and the continuation of "Two Orphans" translated by Bo Yi, these two issues of "The Vanguard Weekly" also include "Bloodstains" (essays) by Xiang Hua, "Miss Anjin" (fiction) by Guan Li, two book and newspaper reviews by Jin Xuan: (1) "The Short Story Magazine," July issue; (2) inaugural issue of "The Vanguard Monthly," and Jin Xuan's commentary on "Nothing." These are all rare masterpieces. It is truly commendable to be able to discuss exceptionally precise literary theory and creation in such a small publication. Now, I will analyze it from a literary perspective as follows.

Prefacing the two issues is the "On Nationalist Literary Poetry," seemingly an unfinished long treatise in form. However, after reading these two sections, we deeply understand the correctness of the author's argument and the richness of the content. The author provides a more detailed analysis and explanation of the significance of nationalist poetry, stating at the outset: "We can know that nationalist poetry already contained a full embryo in ancient China, and it did not begin with the present advocacy of the nationalist literary movement. In fact, we know that the emergence of any literary trend has its long historical background and the needs of the current social objective environment. As the highest artistic expression, poetry already possessed a strong national consciousness in primitive times. At present, China is suffering from the double oppression of imperialism, and the nation is withering to the extreme. Due to objective demands, nationalist poetry is bound to emerge." Words with such profound insights are indeed of great significance, but every sentence and word is based on historical facts and cannot be compared to other empty theories. Secondly, it lists the understandings of poetry from various schools, both ancient and modern, Chinese and foreign. At the same time, after each definition, it adds a general explanation and uses a nationalist perspective to make a stern critique. Finally, it synthesizes the significance of nationalist poetry to obtain a clear indication, confirming several essential elements of nationalist poetry: (A) General national consciousness; (B) General ideas that promote national upward development; (C) The actual lives of the national people; (D) The principle of free rhythm. Although these four points proposed by the author with precision cannot be regarded as definite definitions, they have answered the conditions that nationalist poetry should possess.

Secondly, it describes the content of nationalist poetry. The author also provides a detailed explanation based on the important elements in poetry, such as thought, emotion, and imagination. In the content of nationalist poetry, the most important is, of course, thought. Therefore, the author states, "The most important thing in the content of poetry is, of course, thought. However, in expressing nationalist poetry, all ideas that hinder the upward progress of the nation, especially those so-called incorrect proletarian consciousness, should be excluded. In addition, otherworldly thoughts, religious thoughts, sentimental thoughts, decadent thoughts, romantic thoughts, feudal thoughts, etc., should not be incorporated into our poetry." We only need to look at this section on thought to understand the author's following exposition on emotion and imagination. Finally, the author describes the content of nationalist poetry as composed of three things: (1) the central idea of nationalism; (2) the emotion of national consciousness, which is further divided into two types: (A) social emotion; (Social　Emotion) (B) national sentiment; (National　Sentiment) (3) the creative imagination of nationalism. This is indeed a basic and important treatise on nationalist poetry.

Xiang Hua's essay "Bloodstains" is full of deep poetic meaning. The author's artistic skills are exceptionally humorous. In short words, it truly gives us supreme deep taste. The content describes a picture given by a friend, instructing him (referring to the author himself) to preserve it dearly, because it is the meaning contained in the achievements left by our predecessors. The author has already announced to us here, but what follows is many brilliant and unique points, containing infinite deep meaning, truly making us chew on it like delicious olives. After reading this article, we naturally and spontaneously recall the achievements left by our predecessors, a bloodstain.

"The pioneers are smiling and waving to me. I am treading on their bloodstains and rushing towards that solid fortress."

"I would like to add a little bit to this vivid bloodstain picture." This is the author's final description, which can reflect the strength of the entire article. Truly, "Bloodstains" is an extremely humorous essay.

Guan Li's short story "Miss Anjin" is equally worthy of our attention. In this creation, we can also see that the subject matter of nationalist literature has explored the social strata of other oppressed nations. In fact, this is also giving an important explanation to those who distort, insult, blindly scold, and do not understand literary trends. This is certainly clearer than explaining it with basic theories.

Miss Anjin is a woman from the oppressed Korean nation, not very old, about twenty-two or three years old. However, she has been resisting Japanese imperialism and striving for national movement for ten years. At the same time, the tragic death of her parents further accelerated her national movement. As a result, she finally bid farewell to the pitiful Korean people while awakening and encouraging songs. The author's descriptive skills are very strong. A lively pen focuses on Miss Anjin alone. Therefore, even when she was quickly dragged to the execution ground by those Japanese imperialists, it still depicts her explosive and powerful cry: "Arise, oppressed nations, unite! The Korean nation…" Generally speaking, such subject matter is very much needed in China, because our Chinese nation is also oppressed. The entire article is very compact and is indeed a good work.

Publication News 196

The "Banking Weekly," Volume 14, Issue 43, was published yesterday. The content is rich. The main articles include "Rescuing the Silver Price and Reforming the Monetary System (Part 1)," "A Study of American Financial Administration and Accounting System (Part 1)," "Speculation on the Silver Price Panic," "Rationalization of German Banking Operations (4)," "Organization and Functions of International Banks (6)," "Explanatory Statement of the Shanghai Cost of Living Index (Part 2)," "Discussion of Banking Issues," etc. Also, the "Economic Statistics for October" has also been published and sent with the newspaper.

"A Diplomatic History of the National Government" compiled by Hong Junpei of Yixing, has recently been published by Huatong Bookstore. The content is very rich, divided into sixteen chapters, describing the Shashki Incident, the Tariff Conference, the protest against the Hantao case, the Sino-Japanese incident in Hankou, the process of recovering concessions, the cancellation of the Russian recognition case, the Nanjing Incident, the Jinan Incident, the Middle East Road case, etc., without leaving anything out. It is truly a must-read for those concerned about diplomatic issues.

Manuscript Guidelines for Book and Newspaper Introductions 197

(1) The text is limited to introducing the latest publications at home and abroad. In addition to literary works, all books and newspapers on politics, economics, law, science, education, etc., are included. However, vulgar books and newspapers will not be introduced.

(2) Pictures are limited to recent works by new domestic and foreign artists and accompanying illustrations related to the text.

(3) Texts on textual research or research on ancient Chinese books will not be published.

(4) Books and photos are limited to those related to the text.

(5) Each text should not exceed 3,000 characters at most.

Art World

Reflections After Viewing the Japanese Children's Art Exhibition 198

— Chen Baoyi

I recently visited the "National Japanese Children's Excellent Painting Exhibition" held at the Kimura Company (art supplies and stationery store) on Wusong Road twice. The works on display were mainly crayon paintings (detailed later), crayon drawings, and watercolors. Although in that small exhibition venue, amid not-so-perfect displays, those paintings created by tender hearts and hands did indeed greatly stimulate my interest. At the same time, I was able to glimpse the development of artistic concepts among Japanese elementary school children in recent years.

Originally, this exhibition had already been held in Japan. This was the fourth national children's painting exhibition, mainly sponsored by the Ousama Trading Company and Sakura Trading Company. It is a regular practice to solicit works from children nationwide every year.

This time, 121,905 pieces were collected in Japan, of which only 1,600 were selected. The strictness of the selection can be seen. Only a part of this shipment was brought to Shanghai for the exhibition, only 123 pieces selected from over a thousand. This time, it was originally intended to give Japanese children in Shanghai the opportunity to compare and study the achievements of children's paintings in their home country, so it was specially transported for exhibition.

Although they are children's paintings, they are extremely careful in their handling of the appraisal. Therefore, the review is solemnly entrusted to first-class masters in the art world and art education field.

The reviewers are Yamamoto Kanae, Nakagawa Kigen, Ishii Hakutei, Adachi Gen'ichiro, Kimura Shohachi, Nabeshima Katsuyuki, Harada Ryuutei, Sakui Yasaburo, Yamagata Hiroshi, Otake Sesso, Ishikawa Toraji, Itakura Sanji, Yokoi Soichiro, Itagaki Shigeki, and Sato Heitaro.

Moreover, each piece of work has a label attached with the seals of the reviewers, and the reviewers' thoughts or comments are written on the excellent works to show their responsibility in the review. The sponsoring organization gives considerable rewards to the excellent works to encourage them.

Children's art exhibitions have been frequently held in Japan in recent years. The organizers are various, including the education sector itself, as well as newspaper companies and other cultural organizations, all of whom are enthusiastically involved. It is said that the Ousama Trading Company (a company that sells painting supplies) alone has recently collected more than 230,000 children's paintings. This alone is enough to get a glimpse of its vigorous state.

Speaking of the paintings I saw at this exhibition, most of them were crayon paintings, crayon drawings, and watercolors. There were many excellent works in the whole collection, so I cannot record them in detail one by one. I can only describe the general trend.

Although they are the achievements of elementary school students, they are expressed innocently and clearly, showcasing a "display of the children's world." There are descriptions of sports meets, children's games, processions of soldiers, scenes seen on the streets, scenery of villages and gardens, characters and lives in families, school scenes, snow-covered villages, city roads, buildings, flowers, fruits, etc. The subjects are depicted in extremely freehand ways with very bright colors—vibrant yellow, red, azure blue, deep green, tea brown, peach red, black and white. The works have a very varied color palette, vividly expressing their impressions of the world. In particular, the unique drawing method of crayon paintings skillfully utilized by their small hands is very interesting.

"Craypas" is a type of pigment pen created in Japan in recent years. Its properties have the characteristics of both "colored crayons" and "pastels" (colored chalk). It can also be said to be a type of nearly solid oil painting pigment, shaped like a pastel tube. It is free and convenient to use, but it is better than traditional colored pencils and crayons. This type of craypas can produce the following effects:

Rich and smooth lines

Gradations that can be freely blended

Free mixing of colors

Bright colors can be added on top of dark colors

It can also be mixed and thinned with turpentine oil to create a variety of mixed colors.

Some of the paintings I saw this time had fine lines scratched out on the thickly applied craypas color to create patterns. I think this shows an unusually interesting effect in the depiction of patterns or decorations.

Overall, I am impressed by several points of the Japanese children's paintings this time: the vivid expression of impressions, the free expression of subjects based directly on children's own perceptions, and the "freedom" and "abundance" in the application of expression techniques. This can indeed prove the progress of Japanese elementary school art education in recent years.

However, the reason why it can tend toward this trend and develop is not without reason. Looking back at its origin, it seems that we have to go back to the "Free Painting Movement" advocated by Western-style painter Yamamoto Kanae ten years ago.

Originally, Japanese elementary schools were still practicing model education. Elementary school students learned to draw by copying models. In fact, this method can only make children practice pen techniques mechanically and cannot inspire children's perceptions of "nature" expression. Although there are occasional sketching courses, they are based on the traditional, rigid teaching method. At most, they can copy the model to the fullest extent, but they cannot allow children's own perceptions and personalities to be expressed in creation. At that time, Mr. Yamamoto keenly pointed out various malpractices and vigorously advocated reform movements. For a time, there were many who resonated with him, and opponents also showed considerable momentum. At that time, various discussions were aroused in the education sector. However, after all, this matter concerned the life and death of the future of art education, so they did not hesitate to work hard to overcome difficulties. Mr. Yamamoto wrote a long article to thoroughly refute all the opponents. At that time, he and Katagami Shinn, Kishibe Fukuou, Kitahara Hakushu, Nagahara Kotaro, Tani Yoshio, Kanai Masayama, Yamagoe Kyozo, and others organized the "Japan Children's Free Painting Association." Later, they also published the magazine "Artistic Free Education" to vigorously innovate the methods of children's art research. At that time, major news agencies all gave unusual support to the repercussions and held exhibitions in various places to promote this movement.

The first "Free Painting Exhibition" to be piloted was held in April of Taisho 8 (i.e., 1919) at the Kanzawa Elementary School in Kamikawa Village, Agata County, Mr. Yamamoto's hometown. Before long, sympathizers successively opened several exhibitions in various places such as north and south Shinano, Tokyo, Tanba, Kyushu, and Osaka. At that time, the Tokyo Nichinichi Shimbun hosted a "Japan Children's Free Painting Exhibition." Subsequently, the Osaka Asahi Shimbun also hosted a "World Children's Free Painting Exhibition." Moreover, the Asahi Shimbun used the summer lecture to carry out exhibitions and lectures in a total of thirty-five places across Japan and worked hard to promote its beautiful intentions. This was the situation at that time.

In less than a few years, this reality has naturally been discovered in elementary school education throughout Japan. Since then, children's self-painting exhibitions have been commonly held. However, now there seems to be no need to mention the words "free painting," and people know that this method is pure and reasonable for inspiring children's artistic perceptions.

Seeing their current achievements, one cannot help but think of Mr. Yamamoto Kanae's contributions. Although many people do not pay much attention to this matter, it has given a great contribution to children's art education, which cannot be denied. Mr. Yamamoto was originally an artist and not an elementary school art teacher, but in terms of this matter, that is, the reform of art education, he is indeed a pioneer. The good influence of Western-style painting development can also be seen here in the art education given to elementary schools.

Here, I will try to record a part of the prospectus of the intent of Mr. Yamamoto Kanae's advocacy of "free painting" at the time (late March of Taisho year).

The children's painting education practiced in various elementary schools has generally been "copying paintings" and "sketching" methods. Here, what I call "free painting" refers to including sketching, memory, imagination, etc., that is, directly expressing the work without the help of a copybook.

According to the traditional teachings, children spend more time copying adults' paintings that have undergone crude printing than directly absorbing shapes or colors from "nature." I think this is wrong. Why? Because, for example, a meaningless line on a copybook will have the same power as a beautiful and living tree line in the minds of children. They will honestly imitate whatever reason there is. Therefore, if withered copybooks are given to them to copy, the eyes and hands of the children will also become withered. If the eyes of children are guided to the abundant "natural world," their hearts and hands will naturally become lively. This is also proven by the fact that the paintings of children before school age are generally active and vivid.

In short, in children's painting, it is wrong to encourage them to have the same observation power as adults. Children must be allowed to open up, especially in terms of artistic cultivation.

"Beauty," if it only relies on the cultivation of teachers, will eventually fall. It is our task to completely take back the future of Japanese art and art crafts from the hands of those old-fashioned artists who seem to have been created from copybooks and powder books. After all, what is the purpose of teaching writing, painting, and music together with arithmetic, geography, etc. in elementary schools?

It goes without saying that it is to cultivate noble human-like "beauty" sentiments along with knowledge. If all human beings have strong physical strength, bright appearance, sufficient knowledge, and suitable careers, isn't that a remarkable phenomenon? Moreover, if everyone is a poet, an artist, and a musician in their sentiments, then it is truly heaven. Although such a heaven is like a "fool's dream" among us today, no one would rather go in the opposite direction, just as no one is willing to go towards poverty.

In short, people are constantly seeking "creation" and "nurturing." Where these two progress extremely smoothly, one cannot help but feel that a healthy culture can be built.

I aim to make our art and art crafts the smoothest things and feel that this matter really must start from children's painting education.

As mentioned above, I think we must sweep away the malpractice of adults only copying powder books and children only copying copybooks. Instead of giving copybooks to children to imitate, it is better to freely graze them in "nature" and force them to produce. This matter will also be of interest to adults, but it is also meaningful to children. Children, in relation to "nature," will probably create paintings directly on the one hand and independently understand "beauty" on the other hand. "Beauty" is indeed something of this nature, just as sexuality will also imply in the chest of a young man without giving him a copybook. This is the secret of nature and the profound wisdom of the gods. (Omitted below)

In addition, there are various documents on free painting education. Mr. Yamamoto's "Key Points of Free Painting Education" is exceptionally clear and will be introduced again at the next opportunity.

As for how the achievements of elementary school art education in our country are in recent years, I naturally cannot have a definite judgment because I have never studied it.

However, regarding this aspect, I can also base it on having seen the painting achievements of many elementary school students and according to what friends who are directly teaching elementary school students have told me about the situation of elementary school art classes. Moreover, two or three years ago, I taught art classes at the attached middle school of a certain university for a few months, so I can also infer one aspect of the art achievements in recent years. According to this situation, I know that most elementary schools in our country are still copying copybooks perfunctorily with one or two small books. Moreover, schools do not seem to pay much attention to this curriculum. Also, due to the set time, equipment, and teaching, etc., it does not seem to make students feel any interest.

According to the situation of the art class in the attached middle school that I directly saw, although the middle school also had several plaster statues and models, it was also strange that there was no art classroom. Students had never had any cultivation in even the simplest sketching. How they learned to paint in the schools they had attended before, they said that the teachers only taught us to copy copybooks or to copy some pictures cut out from magazines. They only had such experience, so it was very difficult for them to start learning to sketch from scratch in the one-hour art class each week. Even the students found it exceptionally difficult and did not know where to start when facing the real thing. One of them saw the real thing for sketching and said, "How can you teach us to draw like this, teacher? Why don't you draw it on the blackboard for us to copy?" This statement shows nothing but his imitative habits since elementary school. I didn't feel it was particularly strange either because they had never had any beneficial training in art classes in their previous preparations in elementary school. All they had was perfunctory copying for one or two hours. Therefore, they couldn't draw anything without a drawing book. This habit seems to be prevalent in elementary schools in our country. This is not surprising because most elementary schools seem to arrange the art curriculum as usual. As for why they want to inspire children's "sense of beauty" and how to inspire their expression of the sense of beauty, it seems that they have never considered it, and they don't even know the necessity of this consideration. Therefore, many students perfunctorily deal with this curriculum, and the school authorities do not criticize any situation and think that it is just a general communication.

Originally, the purpose of the school's art class was not to train children to be professional artists. Its purpose was nothing more than to inspire children's "sense of beauty," that is, to make them wake up their beautiful eyes and minds to the beauty of phenomena, shapes, and colors, to make them perceive and express according to their own perceptions, and to induce their "creative feelings." This was originally not to train them to be painters.

Even so, how much effectiveness can the copybook teaching method have for this purpose? I think that the copybook can only unconditionally make children copy some strokes, and its image is not something that originates from the interest of the copying child himself. No matter how well (or like) it is copied, it is not enough to prove that it is the experience of the child's own sense of beauty. He probably cannot discover anything that he has perceived himself, so he does not reveal his emotions about the phenomenon. Therefore, copying can only make students develop the habit of imitation and cannot make students generate creative attempts. Therefore, there is a reason why drawing models cannot serve as art guidance here. Good drawing models can at most serve as a reference for students, but they cannot be directly given to students to copy.

Children have their own feelings and observations. Teachers can only guide them well, give instructions and explanations, and make their perception power be inspired, but they cannot make them blindly follow the works of teachers or models. In short, directly trying and inspiring students' perception power is to make students grasp a kind of power. Therefore, directly facing the real thing (or the real phenomenon) and making them freely describe it according to their own perceptions is to make this power natural and expanded.

This method is not to encourage their depictions to tend toward strangeness, but to inspire the perception power that they each have and to make them naturally express it according to pure love.

Among the middle and primary schools in our country, there are probably some schools that truly handle art classes well, but it seems that only a few. In the past, most educators in our country thought that the art class was dispensable, so how the achievements are is not what they want to ask about. Moreover, there are many people who only think of children's painting as a waste of time without thinking about the great relationship between the study of painting and children's sense of beauty. These situations are also because the education sector in our country has not sincerely paid attention to the matter of "art education." I hope that all those who work hard directly in art education will innovate in this area in the future to make it develop.

Finally, as a side note, here is a piece of news: recently, the Japanese side in Shanghai, considering that children in Shanghai lack an art environment and cultivation, specially advocated their painting creation. The "All Shanghai Children's Free Painting Exhibition" will be hosted by the "Shanghai Mainichi Shimbun." The first exhibition will be held on the 15th and 16th of this month (November) in the auditorium upstairs of the newspaper (corner of Wusong Road and Tang En Road). (The collection of works will be from the 5th to the 10th, and the review will be carried out by Japanese painter Ogawa Ando and others.) The results cannot be predicted, but I hope that people who pay attention to children's art education can get some references at this opportunity.

Literary Anecdotes

The First Works of Literary Giants 199

It is very interesting to compare the circumstances under which various literary giants published their first works.

Strindberg - At the age of twenty (1870), he wrote the scripts "Hermione" and "The Wanderer," which immediately attracted people's attention. King Charles XV of Sweden specially awarded him an annuity to encourage him. At this time, he entered the school that he had stopped due to poverty, but soon Charles XV died and the annuity also stopped, so he had to fall into extreme poverty again.

Hauptmann - He didn't publish his first long narrative poem "Prometheschulos" until he was thirty-three years old, but he knew that his poems were not yet mature, so he argued in the bookstore to sell them, and then collected and burned all the leftovers. Four years later, he published his masterpiece "Before Sunrise."

Artsybashev - In 1901, he wrote his first work "Pasha Zhamarov," which wrote about the real events of an old abandoned school and hinted at his hatred. This work was approved by a famous review magazine and promised to publish it, but at that time, the inspectors routinely prohibited works about schools, so this work had to be shelved. However, the editor was very surprised by his career and encouraged him to start writing a second work. At this time, he realized his initial dream of becoming an artist and devoted himself to literature.

Ibsen - At the age of fourteen, he was an apprentice in a pharmacy. He lived well for ten years. In 1848, when he was twenty years old, the revolutionary ideas of Europe were very popular, which greatly influenced his young heart. At this time, he was just a pharmacy apprentice, and he worked every night under the candle to write in praise of the revolution. In this way, he wrote his first work, the three-act tragedy "Catalina." In 1849, he left the pharmacy and began to volunteer as an artist. At the age of twenty-two, he got his friend Schuler's help to publish his "Catalina" under the anonymous name "Brynjolf Barm" at his own expense, but at that time, only thirty copies were sold. (Plain)
